    <meta name="dc.description" content="Syria&#8217;s coastal and nearshore zone contains a significant, but under-researched, record of maritime cultural heritage (MCH) ranging from prehistory to the present. This is exemplified by a lack of underwater investigations, but also limited investigation of key onshore maritime sites such as ports and harbours. There is also a lack of specialist in-country management regarding maritime cultural heritage research and protection. This situation has been worsened by the ongoing conflict (since 2011), which has reduced (already limited) field investigation. To assist in the advancement of Syrian maritime archaeology, this paper presents a baseline assessment which makes use of a geospatial database generated from satellite imagery as well as both published and grey literature. This assessment reviews past coastal environment changes affecting the Syrian littoral, and then highlights past disturbances and potential future threats impacting the MCH. This is done through analysis of all coastal/nearshore sites documented to date and showcased in more detail using two case studies: Tabbat al-Hammam and Ras Ibn Hani. This enables discussion of the current state of Syrian maritime archaeology and suggests ways forward for its future management and investigation."/>

    <meta property="og:url" content="https://link.springer.com/article/10.1007/s11457-022-09339-y"/>
    <meta property="og:type" content="article"/>
    <meta property="og:site_name" content="SpringerLink"/>
    <meta property="og:title" content="Maritime Cultural Heritage, Coastal Change and Threat Assessment in Syria - Journal of Maritime Archaeology"/>
    <meta property="og:description" content="Syria&#8217;s coastal and nearshore zone contains a significant, but under-researched, record of maritime cultural heritage (MCH) ranging from prehistory to the present. This is exemplified by a lack of underwater investigations, but also limited investigation of key onshore maritime sites such as ports and harbours. There is also a lack of specialist in-country management regarding maritime cultural heritage research and protection. This situation has been worsened by the ongoing conflict (since 2011), which has reduced (already limited) field investigation. To assist in the advancement of Syrian maritime archaeology, this paper presents a baseline assessment which makes use of a geospatial database generated from satellite imagery as well as both published and grey literature. This assessment reviews past coastal environment changes affecting the Syrian littoral, and then highlights past disturbances and potential future threats impacting the MCH. This is done through analysis of all coastal/nearshore sites documented to date and showcased in more detail using two case studies: Tabbat al-Hammam and Ras Ibn Hani. This enables discussion of the current state of Syrian maritime archaeology and suggests ways forward for its future management and investigation."/>
    <meta property="og:image" content="https://static-content.springer.com/image/art%3A10.1007%2Fs11457-022-09339-y/MediaObjects/11457_2022_9339_Fig1_HTML.png"/>

    <script type="application/ld+json">{"mainEntity":{"headline":"Maritime Cultural Heritage, Coastal Change and Threat Assessment in Syria","description":"Syria’s coastal and nearshore zone contains a significant, but under-researched, record of maritime cultural heritage (MCH) ranging from prehistory to the present. This is exemplified by a lack of underwater investigations, but also limited investigation of key onshore maritime sites such as ports and harbours. There is also a lack of specialist in-country management regarding maritime cultural heritage research and protection. This situation has been worsened by the ongoing conflict (since 2011), which has reduced (already limited) field investigation. To assist in the advancement of Syrian maritime archaeology, this paper presents a baseline assessment which makes use of a geospatial database generated from satellite imagery as well as both published and grey literature. This assessment reviews past coastal environment changes affecting the Syrian littoral, and then highlights past disturbances and potential future threats impacting the MCH. This is done through analysis of all coastal/nearshore sites documented to date and showcased in more detail using two case studies: Tabbat al-Hammam and Ras Ibn Hani. This enables discussion of the current state of Syrian maritime archaeology and suggests ways forward for its future management and investigation.","datePublished":"2022-09-13T00:00:00Z","dateModified":"2022-09-13T00:00:00Z","pageStart":"353","pageEnd":"373","license":"http://creativecommons.org/licenses/by/4.0/","sameAs":"https://doi.org/10.1007/s11457-022-09339-y","keywords":["Maritime </li><li class="c-article-author-list__show-more" aria-label="Show all 5 authors for this article" title="Show all 5 authors for this article">…</li><li class="c-article-author-list__item"><a data-test="author-name" data-track="click" data-track-action="open author" data-track-label="link" href="#auth-Lucy-Blue-Aff5" data-author-popup="auth-Lucy-Blue-Aff5" data-author-search="Blue, Lucy">Lucy Blue</a><sup class="u-js-hide"><a href="#Aff5">5</a></sup> </li></ul><button aria-expanded="false" class="c-article-author-list__button"><svg width="16" height="16" focusable="false" role="img" aria-hidden="true" class="u-icon"><use xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xlink:href="#icon-eds-i-chevron-down-medium"></use></svg><span>Show authors</span></button>

                <div data-article-body="true" data-track-component="article body" class="c-article-body">
                    <section aria-labelledby="Abs1" data-title="Abstract" lang="en"><div class="c-article-section" id="Abs1-section"><h2 class="c-article-section__title js-section-title js-c-reading-companion-sections-item" id="Abs1">Abstract</h2><div class="c-article-section__content" id="Abs1-content"><p>Syria’s coastal and nearshore zone contains a significant, but under-researched, record of maritime cultural heritage (MCH) ranging from prehistory to the present. This is exemplified by a lack of underwater investigations, but also limited investigation of key onshore maritime sites such as ports and harbours. There is also a lack of specialist in-country management regarding maritime cultural heritage research and protection. This situation has been worsened by the ongoing conflict (since 2011), which has reduced (already limited) field investigation. To assist in the advancement of Syrian maritime archaeology, this paper presents a baseline assessment which makes use of a geospatial database generated from satellite imagery as well as both published and grey literature. This assessment reviews past coastal environment changes affecting the Syrian littoral, and then highlights past disturbances and potential future threats impacting the MCH. This is done through analysis of all coastal/nearshore sites documented to date and showcased in more detail using two case studies: Tabbat al-Hammam and Ras Ibn Hani. This enables discussion of the current state of Syrian maritime archaeology and suggests ways forward for its future management and investigation.</p></div></div></section>

                                <div class="main-content">
                                    <section data-title="Introduction"><div class="c-article-section" id="Sec1-section"><h2 class="c-article-section__title js-section-title js-c-reading-companion-sections-item" id="Sec1">Introduction</h2><div class="c-article-section__content" id="Sec1-content"><p>The Syrian coastal zone has a rich cultural heritage with archaeological sites ranging from Palaeolithic flint scatters to Bronze and Iron Age occupation mounds (tells) and from Crusader fortifications to Ottoman buildings (Riis et al. <a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 2004" title="Riis PJ, Thuesen I, Lund J, Thomas R (2004) Topographical studies in the Gabla plain (Publications of the Carlsberg Expedition to Phoenicia 13; Det Kongelige Danske Videnskabernes Selskab Historik-filosofiske Skrifter 28). The Royal Danish Academy of Sciences and Letters, Copenhagen" href="/article/10.1007/s11457-022-09339-y#ref-CR60" id="ref-link-section-d69796620e487">2004</a>; Burns <a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 2009" title="Burns R (2009) The monuments of Syria: a guide. IB Tauris, London" href="/article/10.1007/s11457-022-09339-y#ref-CR20" id="ref-link-section-d69796620e490">2009</a>). This area played a major role in trade and seafaring from at least the Bronze Age onwards with ancient textual records describing seaborne military or commercial activity involving ports and harbours on the Syrian coast. Archaeological evidence of these activities also exists as the material remains of port facilities, ranging from modified natural harbours to man-made structures such as moles and quays (Carayon <a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 2008" title="Carayon N (2008) Les Ports Pheniciens et Puniques. Geomorphologie et Infrastructure. PhD Thesis" href="/article/10.1007/s11457-022-09339-y#ref-CR23" id="ref-link-section-d69796620e493">2008</a>).</p><p>In 1992, the Syrian Navy officer, Hussein Hijazi published the results of his extensive fieldwork, conducted over twenty years, on the Syrian coastline in his book <i>Ancient Ports, Harbours and Anchorages Along the Syrian Coast.</i> This was the first systematic mapping and recording of the ports, harbours, shipwrecks, and settlements on the Syrian coastline based on detailed field observations and architectural descriptions (Hijazi <a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 1992" title="Hijazi H (1992) Ancient ports, harbors and anchorages on the Syrian coast. Sham Printing Press, Damascus" href="/article/10.1007/s11457-022-09339-y#ref-CR41" id="ref-link-section-d69796620e502">1992</a>). However, aside from this study, the maritime aspects of this cultural heritage remain under-researched. One illustration of this is the lack of underwater archaeological work (Haldane <a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 1993" title="Haldane D (1993) At the crossroads of history: nautical archaeology in Syria. Inst Nautical Archaeol Quarterly 20:7–11" href="/article/10.1007/s11457-022-09339-y#ref-CR37" id="ref-link-section-d69796620e505">1993</a>; Kampbell <a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 2013" title="Kampbell SM (2013) Shipwrecks of the Syrian coast. Int J Naut Archaeol 42:419–425. &#xA;                  https://doi.org/10.1111/1095-9270.12004&#xA;                  &#xA;                " href="/article/10.1007/s11457-022-09339-y#ref-CR44" id="ref-link-section-d69796620e508">2013</a>) compared to the extensive shipwreck, harbour and (to a lesser extent) submerged landscape investigations conducted elsewhere in the eastern Mediterranean (Demesticha <a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 2018" title="Demesticha S (2018) Cutting a Long Story Short?: underwater and maritime archaeology in Cyprus. J Eastern Mediterranean Archaeol Heritage Studies 6:62–78. &#xA;                  https://doi.org/10.5325/jeasmedarcherstu.6.1-2.0062&#xA;                  &#xA;                " href="/article/10.1007/s11457-022-09339-y#ref-CR28" id="ref-link-section-d69796620e511">2018</a>; Galili et al. <a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 2018" title="Galili E, Ronen A, Mienis HK, Horwitz LK (2018) Beach deposits containing Middle Paleolithic archaeological remains from northern Israel. Quatern Int 464:43–57. &#xA;                  https://doi.org/10.1016/j.quaint.2017.05.002&#xA;                  &#xA;                " href="/article/10.1007/s11457-022-09339-y#ref-CR35" id="ref-link-section-d69796620e515">2018</a>; Harpster <a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 2018" title="Harpster M (2018) Maritime archaeology in the Eastern Mediterranean: approaches, perspectives, and histories. J Eastern Mediterranean Archaeol Heritage Studies 6:59–61. &#xA;                  https://doi.org/10.5325/jeasmedarcherstu.6.1-2.0059&#xA;                  &#xA;                " href="/article/10.1007/s11457-022-09339-y#ref-CR39" id="ref-link-section-d69796620e518">2018</a>; Semaan <a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 2018" title="Semaan L (2018) Maritime archaeology in the developing world: the case of Lebanon. J Eastern Mediterranean Archaeol Heritage Studies 6:79–98. &#xA;                  https://doi.org/10.5325/jeasmedarcherstu.6.1-2.0079&#xA;                  &#xA;                " href="/article/10.1007/s11457-022-09339-y#ref-CR64" id="ref-link-section-d69796620e521">2018</a>; Demesticha et al. <a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 2019" title="Demesticha S, Semaan L, Morsy Z (2019) Capacity building in maritime archaeology: the case of the Eastern Mediterranean (Cyprus, Lebanon and Egypt). J Marit Archaeol 14:369–389. &#xA;                  https://doi.org/10.1007/s11457-019-09243-y&#xA;                  &#xA;                " href="/article/10.1007/s11457-022-09339-y#ref-CR29" id="ref-link-section-d69796620e524">2019</a>). Onshore, a few coastal sites have been subject to detailed investigation, such as Tell Tweini, Ras Shamra, and Ras el-Bassit (Schaeffer <a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 1929" title="Schaeffer CF-A (1929) Les fouilles de Minet-El-Beida et de Ras Shamra (campagnes du printemps 1929). Syria 10:285–297. &#xA;                  https://doi.org/10.3406/syria.1929.3407&#xA;                  &#xA;                " href="/article/10.1007/s11457-022-09339-y#ref-CR63" id="ref-link-section-d69796620e527">1929</a>; Courbin <a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 1986" title="Courbin P (1986) Bassit Syria 63:175–220. &#xA;                  https://doi.org/10.3406/syria.1986.8665&#xA;                  &#xA;                " href="/article/10.1007/s11457-022-09339-y#ref-CR25" id="ref-link-section-d69796620e530">1986</a>; Yon <a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 2006" title="Yon M (2006) The City of Ugarit at Tell Ras Shamra. Eisenbrauns, Winona Lake, Ind" href="/article/10.1007/s11457-022-09339-y#ref-CR76" id="ref-link-section-d69796620e534">2006</a>; Bretschneider and van Lerberghe <a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 2008" title="Bretschneider J, van Lerberghe K (2008) In search of Gibala. An archaeological and historical study based on eight seasons of excavations at Tell Tweini (Syria) in the A and C fields (1999–2007). Editorial Ausa, (Eds.) Barcelona" href="/article/10.1007/s11457-022-09339-y#ref-CR16" id="ref-link-section-d69796620e537">2008</a>; Al-Maqdissi et al. <a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 2010" title="Al-Maqdissi M, Calvet Y, Matoïan V et al (2010) Rapport préliminaire sur les activités de la mission syro-française de Ras Shamra-Ougarit en 2007 et 2008 (67e et 68e campagnes). Syria 87:21–51. &#xA;                  https://doi.org/10.4000/syria.643&#xA;                  &#xA;                " href="/article/10.1007/s11457-022-09339-y#ref-CR3" id="ref-link-section-d69796620e540">2010</a>; Beaudry <a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 2014" title="Beaudry N (2014) Ras el Bassit and the Late Antique Archaeological Landscape of Coastal North Syria. In: Aitken EB, Fossey JM (eds) The Levant: Crossroads of Late Antiquity. Brill, Leiden, pp 225–272" href="/article/10.1007/s11457-022-09339-y#ref-CR7" id="ref-link-section-d69796620e543">2014</a>; Bretschneider and Jans <a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 2019" title="Bretschneider J, Jans G (eds) (2019) About Tell Tweini (Syria): artefacts, ecofacts and landscape : research results of the Belgian Mission. Peeters, Leuven" href="/article/10.1007/s11457-022-09339-y#ref-CR15" id="ref-link-section-d69796620e546">2019</a>), but many more ancient coastal settlements still need to be investigated (Carayon <a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 2008" title="Carayon N (2008) Les Ports Pheniciens et Puniques. Geomorphologie et Infrastructure. PhD Thesis" href="/article/10.1007/s11457-022-09339-y#ref-CR23" id="ref-link-section-d69796620e549">2008</a>). Moreover, the emphasis has often been on the land-based settlements associated with ports rather than the physical form and infrastructure of the ports themselves or, with the exception of Ras Ibn Hani (Marriner et al. <a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 2012" title="Marriner N, Goiran J-P, Geyer B et al (2012) Ancient harbors and Holocene morphogenesis of the Ras Ibn Hani peninsula (Syria). Quatern Res 78:35–49. &#xA;                  https://doi.org/10.1016/j.yqres.2012.03.005&#xA;                  &#xA;                " href="/article/10.1007/s11457-022-09339-y#ref-CR52" id="ref-link-section-d69796620e553">2012</a>) and Tell Tweini (Al-Maqdissi et al. <a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 2007" title="Al-Maqdissi M, Bretschneider J, Degryse P et al (2007) Environmental changes in the Jebleh plain (Syria), Geophysical, Geomorphological, Palynological, Archaeological and Historical Research. Res Antiquae 4:3–10" href="/article/10.1007/s11457-022-09339-y#ref-CR2" id="ref-link-section-d69796620e556">2007</a>; Baeteman and Bogemans <a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 2019" title="Baeteman C, Bogemans F (2019) In Search of a Harbor in the Past Landscapes of Tell Tweini. Identification of sedimentary environments in support of an archaeological issue. In: Bretschneider J, Jans G (eds) About Tell Tweini (Syria): Artefacts, Ecofacts and Landscape Artefacts, Ecofacts and Landscape. Peeters, Leuven, pp 619–639" href="/article/10.1007/s11457-022-09339-y#ref-CR6" id="ref-link-section-d69796620e559">2019</a>), port development in response to landscape and environmental change. This vacuum has historically extended to heritage management. Typically, maritime features, such as ports, harbours or shipwrecks, are not specifically identified in the Syrian Law of Antiquities (1963), which also only has a passing reference to the offshore remit of the Directorate-General for Antiquities and Museums (DGAM) in relation to issuing archaeological prospection licences. At the time of writing, Syria had not ratified the UNESCO (UNESCO <a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 2001" title="UNESCO (2001) Convention on the protection of the underwater cultural heritage, UNESCO, Paris. &#xA;                  http://www.unesco.org/new/en/culture/themes/underwater-cultural-heritage/&#xA;                  &#xA;                . Accessed 28 Jul 2021" href="/article/10.1007/s11457-022-09339-y#ref-CR65" id="ref-link-section-d69796620e562">2001</a>) Convention on the Protection of the Underwater Cultural Heritage (United Nations <a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 2021" title="United Nations (2021) Multilateral Treaties Deposited with the Secretary-General, United Nations, New York, as available on. &#xA;                  https://treaties.un.org/pages/showdetails.aspx?objid=08000002802198d9&#xA;                  &#xA;                . Accessed 30 Aug 2021" href="/article/10.1007/s11457-022-09339-y#ref-CR66" id="ref-link-section-d69796620e565">2021</a>). Information from former members of the DGAM also indicated that there had not been a concerted effort to engage with the management of maritime cultural heritage or establish a dedicated maritime archaeology unit (Al-Maqdissi pers. comm.). The lack of maritime archaeological research and management has undoubtedly been hindered by the Syrian conflict, ongoing since 2011, which has prevented relevant capacity building efforts like those that have taken place elsewhere in the eastern Mediterranean over the last decade (Blue and Breen <a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 2019" title="Blue L, Breen C (2019) Maritime archaeology and capacity development in the Global South. J Marit Archaeol 14:321–332. &#xA;                  https://doi.org/10.1007/s11457-019-09244-x&#xA;                  &#xA;                " href="/article/10.1007/s11457-022-09339-y#ref-CR11" id="ref-link-section-d69796620e568">2019</a>; Demesticha et al. <a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 2019" title="Demesticha S, Semaan L, Morsy Z (2019) Capacity building in maritime archaeology: the case of the Eastern Mediterranean (Cyprus, Lebanon and Egypt). J Marit Archaeol 14:369–389. &#xA;                  https://doi.org/10.1007/s11457-019-09243-y&#xA;                  &#xA;                " href="/article/10.1007/s11457-022-09339-y#ref-CR29" id="ref-link-section-d69796620e572">2019</a>).</p><p>Since 2011, much of Syria has been devastated by the ongoing conflict. Over much of the country, violence and illegal looting has had disastrous impacts on cultural heritage (Casana and Laugier <a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 2017" title="Casana J, Laugier EJ (2017) Satellite imagery-based monitoring of archaeological site damage in the Syrian civil war. PLoS ONE 12:e0188589. &#xA;                  https://doi.org/10.1371/journal.pone.0188589&#xA;                  &#xA;                " href="/article/10.1007/s11457-022-09339-y#ref-CR24" id="ref-link-section-d69796620e578">2017</a>; Danti et al. <a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 2017" title="Danti M, Branting S, Penacho S (2017) The American schools of oriental research cultural heritage initiatives: monitoring cultural heritage in Syria and Northern Iraq by geospatial imagery. Geosciences 7:95. &#xA;                  https://doi.org/10.3390/geosciences7040095&#xA;                  &#xA;                " href="/article/10.1007/s11457-022-09339-y#ref-CR27" id="ref-link-section-d69796620e581">2017</a>; Kanjou <a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 2020" title="Kanjou Y (2020) The role of Syrian archaeologists and Foreign archaeological missions in the protection of the Syrian heritage. In: Otto A, Herles M, Kanuith K (Eds.) Proceedings of the 11th International Congress on the Archaeology of the Ancient Near East Volume 1. Harrassowitz Verlag, Wiesbaden, pp 339–350" href="/article/10.1007/s11457-022-09339-y#ref-CR45" id="ref-link-section-d69796620e584">2020</a>). In contrast, the coastal governorates of Tartus and Latakia have remained under government control during the conflict and largely escaped the worst fighting (Mohamed et al. <a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 2020" title="Mohamed MA, Anders J, Schneider C (2020) Monitoring of Changes in Land Use/Land Cover in Syria from 2010 to 2018 using multitemporal landsat imagery and GIS. Land 9:226. &#xA;                  https://doi.org/10.3390/LAND9070226&#xA;                  &#xA;                " href="/article/10.1007/s11457-022-09339-y#ref-CR54" id="ref-link-section-d69796620e587">2020</a>). Even so, other potential threats exist in these coastal areas, for instance population pressure and land use change driven by growing populations of Internally Displaced Persons (IDPs) (Hammad et al. <a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 2018" title="Hammad M, Mucsi L, van Leeuwen B (2018) Land cover change investigation in the Southern Syrian coastal basins during the Past 30-Years using landsat remote sensing data. J Environ Geography 11:45–51. &#xA;                  https://doi.org/10.2478/JENGEO-2018-0006&#xA;                  &#xA;                " href="/article/10.1007/s11457-022-09339-y#ref-CR38" id="ref-link-section-d69796620e590">2018</a>; Mohamed et al. <a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 2020" title="Mohamed MA, Anders J, Schneider C (2020) Monitoring of Changes in Land Use/Land Cover in Syria from 2010 to 2018 using multitemporal landsat imagery and GIS. Land 9:226. &#xA;                  https://doi.org/10.3390/LAND9070226&#xA;                  &#xA;                " href="/article/10.1007/s11457-022-09339-y#ref-CR54" id="ref-link-section-d69796620e594">2020</a>; Mohamed <a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 2021" title="Mohamed MA (2021) An assessment of forest cover change and its driving forces in the Syrian Coastal Region during a Period of Conflict, 2010 to 2020. Land 10:191. &#xA;                  https://doi.org/10.3390/land10020191&#xA;                  &#xA;                " href="/article/10.1007/s11457-022-09339-y#ref-CR53" id="ref-link-section-d69796620e597">2021</a>) and, over the coming years, increased flooding and coastal erosion as a result of anthropogenic climate change (Reimann et al. <a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 2018" title="Reimann L, Vafeidis AT, Brown S et al (2018) Mediterranean UNESCO World Heritage at risk from coastal flooding and erosion due to sea-level rise. Nat Commun 9:4161. &#xA;                  https://doi.org/10.1038/s41467-018-06645-9&#xA;                  &#xA;                " href="/article/10.1007/s11457-022-09339-y#ref-CR58" id="ref-link-section-d69796620e600">2018</a>; Westley et al. <a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 2021" title="Westley K, Andreou G, el Safadi C et al (2021) Climate change and coastal archaeology in the Middle East and North Africa: assessing past impacts and future threats. J Island Coastal Archaeol. &#xA;                  https://doi.org/10.1080/15564894.2021.1955778&#xA;                  &#xA;                " href="/article/10.1007/s11457-022-09339-y#ref-CR71" id="ref-link-section-d69796620e603">2021</a>). In all cases, the impact of these threats will be exacerbated by the breakdown and loss of support for already limited management efforts (Kanjou <a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 2020" title="Kanjou Y (2020) The role of Syrian archaeologists and Foreign archaeological missions in the protection of the Syrian heritage. In: Otto A, Herles M, Kanuith K (Eds.) Proceedings of the 11th International Congress on the Archaeology of the Ancient Near East Volume 1. Harrassowitz Verlag, Wiesbaden, pp 339–350" href="/article/10.1007/s11457-022-09339-y#ref-CR45" id="ref-link-section-d69796620e606">2020</a>).</p><p>To assist in the development of a framework for future investigation and protection of Syria’s maritime cultural heritage, this paper presents an up-to-date baseline assessment. It incorporates a review of past climate and environmental changes affecting the Syrian coast, heritage documentation through remote sensing, and a threat assessment exercise following the methodologies of the Maritime Endangered Archaeology (MarEA) project (Andreou et al. <a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 2020" title="Andreou G, Blue L, Breen C et al (2020) Maritime endangered archaeology of the Middle East and North Africa: the MarEA project. Antiquity 94:e36. &#xA;                  https://doi.org/10.15184/aqy.2020.196&#xA;                  &#xA;                " href="/article/10.1007/s11457-022-09339-y#ref-CR5" id="ref-link-section-d69796620e612">2020</a>) as well as an initial assessment funded by the Honor Frost Foundation (HFF) (Westley et al. <a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 2018" title="Westley K, Carayon N, Breen C, Blue L (2018) Benchmarking the Maritime Cultural Heritage of Syria. Unpublished report prepared for the Honor Frost Foundation" href="/article/10.1007/s11457-022-09339-y#ref-CR72" id="ref-link-section-d69796620e615">2018</a>)<b>.</b> Two case studies (Tabbat al-Hammam, Ras Ibn Hani) are presented and followed by a discussion on the present state of Syrian maritime archaeology and the outlook for its future management and investigation.</p></div></div></section><section data-title="Coastal Climate and Environmental Change"><div class="c-article-section" id="Sec2-section"><h2 class="c-article-section__title js-section-title js-c-reading-companion-sections-item" id="Sec2">Coastal Climate and Environmental Change</h2><div class="c-article-section__content" id="Sec2-content"><p>Past environmental changes along the Syrian coast are integral considerations in heritage management, relevant to site prospection, archaeological interpretation and addressing long-term coastal changes which impact the future management of heritage sites.</p><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec3">Physical Geography and Geology</h3><p>The Syrian coastline is approximately 200 km  long and characterized by a narrow plain running between the sea and the Jabal an Nusayriyah Mountains and which rarely extends inland by more than 1 km except for the Jableh and Akkar Plains (Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/article/10.1007/s11457-022-09339-y#Fig1">1</a>). The plain consists of Quaternary fluvial, colluvial, and marine sediments that accumulated against the western foothills of the mountains which themselves are mainly uplifted Jurassic and Cretaceous carbonates with Cretaceous ophiolites (the Baer-Bassit Massif) and Pliocene basalts to the north and south, respectively (Brew et al. <a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 2001" title="Brew G, Barazangi M, Khaled Al-Maleh A, Sawaf T (2001) Tectonic and geologic evolution of Syria. GeoArabia 6:573–616. &#xA;                  https://doi.org/10.2113/geoarabia0604573a&#xA;                  &#xA;                " href="/article/10.1007/s11457-022-09339-y#ref-CR17" id="ref-link-section-d69796620e639">2001</a>; Dodonov et al. <a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 2008" title="Dodonov AE, Trifonov VG, Ivanova TP et al (2008) Late Quaternary marine terraces in the Mediterranean coastal area of Syria: geochronology and neotectonics. Quatern Int 190:158–170. &#xA;                  https://doi.org/10.1016/j.quaint.2008.02.008&#xA;                  &#xA;                " href="/article/10.1007/s11457-022-09339-y#ref-CR30" id="ref-link-section-d69796620e642">2008</a>).</p><div class="c-article-section__figure js-c-reading-companion-figures-item" data-test="figure" data-container-section="figure" id="figure-1" data-title="Fig. 1"><figure><figcaption><b id="Fig1" class="c-article-section__figure-caption" data-test="figure-caption-text">Fig. 1</b></figcaption><div class="c-article-section__figure-content"><div class="c-article-section__figure-item"><a class="c-article-section__figure-link" data-test="img-link" data-track="click" data-track-label="image" data-track-action="view figure" href="/article/10.1007/s11457-022-09339-y/figures/1" rel="nofollow"><picture><source type="image/webp" srcset="//media.springernature.com/lw685/springer-static/image/art%3A10.1007%2Fs11457-022-09339-y/MediaObjects/11457_2022_9339_Fig1_HTML.png?as=webp"><img aria-describedby="Fig1" src="//media.springernature.com/lw685/springer-static/image/art%3A10.1007%2Fs11457-022-09339-y/MediaObjects/11457_2022_9339_Fig1_HTML.png" alt="figure 1" loading="lazy" width="685" height="690"></picture></a></div><div class="c-article-section__figure-description" data-test="bottom-caption" id="figure-1-desc"><p>Onshore topography (SRTM 30 m elevation model), offshore bathymetry (EMODNET 2020 elevation model) and constituent material of the Syrian coast (Mediterranean coastal database; Wolff et al. <a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 2018" title="Wolff C, Vafeidis AT, Muis S et al (2018) A Mediterranean coastal database for assessing the impacts of sea-level rise and associated hazards. Scientific Data 5:1–11. &#xA;                  https://doi.org/10.1038/sdata.2018.44&#xA;                  &#xA;                " href="/article/10.1007/s11457-022-09339-y#ref-CR73" id="ref-link-section-d69796620e655">2018</a>). LECZ refers to the Low Elevation Coastal Zone (land &lt; 10 m elevation and in hydrological connection to the coast)</p></div></div><div class="u-text-right u-hide-print"><a class="c-article__pill-button" data-test="article-link" data-track="click" data-track-label="button" data-track-action="view figure" href="/article/10.1007/s11457-022-09339-y/figures/1" data-track-dest="link:Figure1 Full size image" aria-label="Full size image figure 1" rel="nofollow"><span>Full size image</span><svg width="16" height="16" focusable="false" role="img" aria-hidden="true" class="u-icon"><use xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xlink:href="#icon-eds-i-chevron-right-small"></use></svg></a></div></figure></div><p>The tidal regime is microtidal (~ 0.3 m range), and coastal geomorphology is accordingly wave-dominated: zones of beachrock and unconsolidated sediment alternate with low rocky shores. Small pocket beaches, bays, promontories, headlands, and estuaries also occur intermittently. Dunes are present on the Akkar Plain and south of Latakia. High coastal cliffs are located in the north where the Baer-Bassit Massif reaches the sea. There are few offshore islands, the largest of which, and only inhabited example, is Arwad Island off the coast of Tartus (Bird <a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 2010" title="Bird E (2010) Encyclopedia of the World’s Coastal Landforms. Springer International Publishing, UK" href="/article/10.1007/s11457-022-09339-y#ref-CR10" id="ref-link-section-d69796620e669">2010</a>; Wolff et al. <a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 2018" title="Wolff C, Vafeidis AT, Muis S et al (2018) A Mediterranean coastal database for assessing the impacts of sea-level rise and associated hazards. Scientific Data 5:1–11. &#xA;                  https://doi.org/10.1038/sdata.2018.44&#xA;                  &#xA;                " href="/article/10.1007/s11457-022-09339-y#ref-CR73" id="ref-link-section-d69796620e672">2018</a>).</p><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec4">Past Relative Sea-Level (RSL) Change</h3><p>During the Pleistocene, global sea-level rose and fell by up to 120 m , relative to the present day, in response to the growth and melting of ice sheets (Rohling et al. <a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 2009" title="Rohling EJ, Grant K, Bolshaw M et al (2009) Antarctic temperature and global sea level closely coupled over the past five glacial cycles. Nat Geosci 2:500–504. &#xA;                  https://doi.org/10.1038/ngeo557&#xA;                  &#xA;                " href="/article/10.1007/s11457-022-09339-y#ref-CR61" id="ref-link-section-d69796620e683">2009</a>). In Syria, this signal was modified by tectonically driven crustal displacement. Consequently, former shorelines and marine terraces have been uplifted to 5–190 m above present sea-level (asl) (Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/article/10.1007/s11457-022-09339-y#Fig2">2</a>A). Their chronology is not yet confirmed, but the lowest terraces probably date to Marine Isotope Stage (MIS) 5 (~ 85–130 ka) and higher examples to one of several highstands ranging from MIS 7 to MIS 11 (~ 190–420 ka) (Besançon et al. <a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 1994" title="Besançon J, Copeland L, Muhesen S, Sanlaville P (1994) Prospection géomorphologique et préhistorique dans la région de Tartous (Syrie). Paléorient 20:5–19. &#xA;                  https://doi.org/10.3406/paleo.1994.4600&#xA;                  &#xA;                " href="/article/10.1007/s11457-022-09339-y#ref-CR9" id="ref-link-section-d69796620e689">1994</a>; Bridgland et al. <a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 2008" title="Bridgland D, Westaway R, Daoud M et al (2008) River Terraces of the Nahr el Kebir, NW Syria, and their Palaeolithic Record. Bull Council British Res Levant 3:41–46. &#xA;                  https://doi.org/10.1179/175272608X375593&#xA;                  &#xA;                " href="/article/10.1007/s11457-022-09339-y#ref-CR18" id="ref-link-section-d69796620e692">2008</a>; Dodonov et al. <a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 2008" title="Dodonov AE, Trifonov VG, Ivanova TP et al (2008) Late Quaternary marine terraces in the Mediterranean coastal area of Syria: geochronology and neotectonics. Quatern Int 190:158–170. &#xA;                  https://doi.org/10.1016/j.quaint.2008.02.008&#xA;                  &#xA;                " href="/article/10.1007/s11457-022-09339-y#ref-CR30" id="ref-link-section-d69796620e695">2008</a>). Although global sea-level fall undoubtedly exposed large swathes of Syria’s continental shelf, evidence of this now submerged palaeolandscape is minimal, probably due to a lack of research. One possible exception is at Ras Ibn Hani where a peat layer representing a former terrestrial landscape now lies buried ~ 4 m below present sea-level (bpsl) and has been radiocarbon dated to 10.4–9.9 ka cal BP (Marriner et al. <a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 2012" title="Marriner N, Goiran J-P, Geyer B et al (2012) Ancient harbors and Holocene morphogenesis of the Ras Ibn Hani peninsula (Syria). Quatern Res 78:35–49. &#xA;                  https://doi.org/10.1016/j.yqres.2012.03.005&#xA;                  &#xA;                " href="/article/10.1007/s11457-022-09339-y#ref-CR52" id="ref-link-section-d69796620e699">2012</a>). Also, at Ras Ibn Hani are potential undated palaeo-channels which have been identified from high-resolution bathymetry (Westley <a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 2021" title="Westley K (2021) Satellite-derived bathymetry for maritime archaeology: testing its effectiveness at two ancient harbors in the Eastern Mediterranean. J Archaeol Sci Rep 38:103030. &#xA;                  https://doi.org/10.1016/J.JASREP.2021.103030&#xA;                  &#xA;                " href="/article/10.1007/s11457-022-09339-y#ref-CR70" id="ref-link-section-d69796620e702">2021</a>). Outside here, there is no published evidence of relative sea-level (RSL) change or submerged landscapes from the Syrian shelf (Benjamin et al. <a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 2017" title="Benjamin J, Rovere A, Fontana A et al (2017) Late Quaternary sea-level changes and early human societies in the central and eastern Mediterranean Basin: an interdisciplinary review. Quatern Int 449:29–57. &#xA;                  https://doi.org/10.1016/j.quaint.2017.06.025&#xA;                  &#xA;                " href="/article/10.1007/s11457-022-09339-y#ref-CR8" id="ref-link-section-d69796620e705">2017</a>).</p><div class="c-article-section__figure js-c-reading-companion-figures-item" data-test="figure" data-container-section="figure" id="figure-2" data-title="Fig. 2"><figure><figcaption><b id="Fig2" class="c-article-section__figure-caption" data-test="figure-caption-text">Fig. 2</b></figcaption><div class="c-article-section__figure-content"><div class="c-article-section__figure-item"><a class="c-article-section__figure-link" data-test="img-link" data-track="click" data-track-label="image" data-track-action="view figure" href="/article/10.1007/s11457-022-09339-y/figures/2" rel="nofollow"><picture><source type="image/webp" srcset="//media.springernature.com/lw685/springer-static/image/art%3A10.1007%2Fs11457-022-09339-y/MediaObjects/11457_2022_9339_Fig2_HTML.png?as=webp"><img aria-describedby="Fig2" src="//media.springernature.com/lw685/springer-static/image/art%3A10.1007%2Fs11457-022-09339-y/MediaObjects/11457_2022_9339_Fig2_HTML.png" alt="figure 2" loading="lazy" width="685" height="1102"></picture></a></div><div class="c-article-section__figure-description" data-test="bottom-caption" id="figure-2-desc"><p><b>a</b> Distribution of dated RSL evidence and identified Pleistocene raised beaches and fluvial terraces in Syria. RSL evidence has been dated by U-series (Dondonov et al. <a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 2008" title="Dodonov AE, Trifonov VG, Ivanova TP et al (2008) Late Quaternary marine terraces in the Mediterranean coastal area of Syria: geochronology and neotectonics. Quatern Int 190:158–170. &#xA;                  https://doi.org/10.1016/j.quaint.2008.02.008&#xA;                  &#xA;                " href="/article/10.1007/s11457-022-09339-y#ref-CR30" id="ref-link-section-d69796620e720">2008</a>) or <sup>14</sup>C (Sanlaville et al. <a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 1997" title="Sanlaville P, Dalongeville R, Berniert P, Evin J (1997) The Syrian coast : a model of holocene coastal evolution. J Coastal Res 13:385–396" href="/article/10.1007/s11457-022-09339-y#ref-CR62" id="ref-link-section-d69796620e725">1997</a>). Raised beaches and fluvial terraces are from Bridgland et al. (<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 2008" title="Bridgland D, Westaway R, Daoud M et al (2008) River Terraces of the Nahr el Kebir, NW Syria, and their Palaeolithic Record. Bull Council British Res Levant 3:41–46. &#xA;                  https://doi.org/10.1179/175272608X375593&#xA;                  &#xA;                " href="/article/10.1007/s11457-022-09339-y#ref-CR18" id="ref-link-section-d69796620e728">2008</a>). The - 100 m contour has been superimposed to give a rough sense of continental shelf exposure during maximal RSL lowstands. <b>b</b> Holocene RSL changes and principal phases of shoreline movement along the Syrian coast (after Sanlaville et al. <a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 1997" title="Sanlaville P, Dalongeville R, Berniert P, Evin J (1997) The Syrian coast : a model of holocene coastal evolution. J Coastal Res 13:385–396" href="/article/10.1007/s11457-022-09339-y#ref-CR62" id="ref-link-section-d69796620e735">1997</a>)</p></div></div><div class="u-text-right u-hide-print"><a class="c-article__pill-button" data-test="article-link" data-track="click" data-track-label="button" data-track-action="view figure" href="/article/10.1007/s11457-022-09339-y/figures/2" data-track-dest="link:Figure2 Full size image" aria-label="Full size image figure 2" rel="nofollow"><span>Full size image</span><svg width="16" height="16" focusable="false" role="img" aria-hidden="true" class="u-icon"><use xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xlink:href="#icon-eds-i-chevron-right-small"></use></svg></a></div></figure></div><p>RSL change and shoreline movement continued into the Holocene as shown by radiocarbon-dated material (bioconstructions, vermetids, shells) from uplifted erosion platforms, beachrock and fossilized coastal dunes. In general, Holocene coastal change comprised three main aggradation phases resulting from increased fluvial sediment input (estimated to be at 5000 BC, 2000 BC, and 1200 AD) and separated by erosive and/or retreat phases and two episodes of coastal uplift in about 1400 BC and 1000 AD. The precise causes of aggradation remain unclear, but could either result from climate changes or human actions, as argued by Ras Ibn Hani (see below) (Sanlaville et al. <a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 1997" title="Sanlaville P, Dalongeville R, Berniert P, Evin J (1997) The Syrian coast : a model of holocene coastal evolution. J Coastal Res 13:385–396" href="/article/10.1007/s11457-022-09339-y#ref-CR62" id="ref-link-section-d69796620e749">1997</a>).</p><p>This general pattern masks a degree of local complexity caused by tectonic movements and sediment supply. These local changes are exemplified by coastal progradation which either created or removed natural harbours. For example, at Amrit the Hellenistic quay and harbour basin are now 800 m inland (Al-Maqdissi <a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 1993" title="Al-Maqdissi M (1993) Chronique des activités archéologiques en Syrie (I). Syria 70:443–560. &#xA;                  https://doi.org/10.3406/syria.1993.7345&#xA;                  &#xA;                " href="/article/10.1007/s11457-022-09339-y#ref-CR1" id="ref-link-section-d69796620e755">1993</a>; Carayon <a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 2008" title="Carayon N (2008) Les Ports Pheniciens et Puniques. Geomorphologie et Infrastructure. PhD Thesis" href="/article/10.1007/s11457-022-09339-y#ref-CR23" id="ref-link-section-d69796620e758">2008</a>)<b>.</b> Another potential infilled natural bay can be identified from declassified Corona spy satellite imagery at Arab al-Milk, close to the site of Tell Daruk, but has yet to be investigated in detail (Westley et al. <a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 2018" title="Westley K, Carayon N, Breen C, Blue L (2018) Benchmarking the Maritime Cultural Heritage of Syria. Unpublished report prepared for the Honor Frost Foundation" href="/article/10.1007/s11457-022-09339-y#ref-CR72" id="ref-link-section-d69796620e764">2018</a>). Conversely, at Ras Ibn Hani, port development was enabled during the Bronze Age when the formation of a tombolo created a sheltered anchorage. In this case, the increase in sediment supply which enabled tombolo formation has been linked to enhanced soil erosion caused by a combination of Bronze and Iron Age agricultural practices and possibly a phase of climatic aridification (Marriner et al. <a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 2012" title="Marriner N, Goiran J-P, Geyer B et al (2012) Ancient harbors and Holocene morphogenesis of the Ras Ibn Hani peninsula (Syria). Quatern Res 78:35–49. &#xA;                  https://doi.org/10.1016/j.yqres.2012.03.005&#xA;                  &#xA;                " href="/article/10.1007/s11457-022-09339-y#ref-CR52" id="ref-link-section-d69796620e767">2012</a>).</p></div></div></section><section data-title="Documentation Results"><div class="c-article-section" id="Sec5-section"><h2 class="c-article-section__title js-section-title js-c-reading-companion-sections-item" id="Sec5">Documentation Results</h2><div class="c-article-section__content" id="Sec5-content"><p>An essential resource for historic environment management is a digital geospatial database of archaeological sites. There has historically been no such database for Syrian maritime sites (Al-Maqdissi pers. comm.). Therefore, a maritime archaeology-focussed database was compiled in 2017–18 by the HFF-sponsored Syria Benchmarking project (Westley et al. <a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 2018" title="Westley K, Carayon N, Breen C, Blue L (2018) Benchmarking the Maritime Cultural Heritage of Syria. Unpublished report prepared for the Honor Frost Foundation" href="/article/10.1007/s11457-022-09339-y#ref-CR72" id="ref-link-section-d69796620e779">2018</a>). This complemented pre-existing and simultaneous documentation in the coastal lowlands by the Endangered Archaeology of the Middle East and North Africa (EAMENA) project (Rayne et al. <a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 2017" title="Rayne L, Bradbury J, Mattingly D et al (2017) From Above and on the ground: geospatial methods for recording endangered archaeology in the Middle East and North Africa. Geosciences 7:100. &#xA;                  https://doi.org/10.3390/geosciences7040100&#xA;                  &#xA;                " href="/article/10.1007/s11457-022-09339-y#ref-CR57" id="ref-link-section-d69796620e782">2017</a>). Since 2019, additional documentation has been undertaken by MarEA (Andreou et al. <a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 2020" title="Andreou G, Blue L, Breen C et al (2020) Maritime endangered archaeology of the Middle East and North Africa: the MarEA project. Antiquity 94:e36. &#xA;                  https://doi.org/10.15184/aqy.2020.196&#xA;                  &#xA;                " href="/article/10.1007/s11457-022-09339-y#ref-CR5" id="ref-link-section-d69796620e785">2020</a>). All these projects used the same method: a combination of literature review and satellite imagery assessment to identify archaeological sites and assess threats and disturbances. All results have been uploaded to the open access EAMENA database hosted at the University of Oxford (<a href="https://database.eamena.org/">https://database.eamena.org/</a>). Key data sources included:</p><ol class="u-list-style-none">
                <li>
                  <span class="u-custom-list-number">(1)</span>
                  
                    <p>Published literature (Braidwood <a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 1940" title="Braidwood RJ (1940) Report on two sondages on the coast of Syria, south of Tartous. Syria 21:183–226. &#xA;                  https://doi.org/10.3406/syria.1940.4189&#xA;                  &#xA;                " href="/article/10.1007/s11457-022-09339-y#ref-CR14" id="ref-link-section-d69796620e805">1940</a>; Besançon et al. <a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 1994" title="Besançon J, Copeland L, Muhesen S, Sanlaville P (1994) Prospection géomorphologique et préhistorique dans la région de Tartous (Syrie). Paléorient 20:5–19. &#xA;                  https://doi.org/10.3406/paleo.1994.4600&#xA;                  &#xA;                " href="/article/10.1007/s11457-022-09339-y#ref-CR9" id="ref-link-section-d69796620e808">1994</a>; Riis et al. <a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 2004" title="Riis PJ, Thuesen I, Lund J, Thomas R (2004) Topographical studies in the Gabla plain (Publications of the Carlsberg Expedition to Phoenicia 13; Det Kongelige Danske Videnskabernes Selskab Historik-filosofiske Skrifter 28). The Royal Danish Academy of Sciences and Letters, Copenhagen" href="/article/10.1007/s11457-022-09339-y#ref-CR60" id="ref-link-section-d69796620e811">2004</a>; Kampbell <a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 2013" title="Kampbell SM (2013) Shipwrecks of the Syrian coast. Int J Naut Archaeol 42:419–425. &#xA;                  https://doi.org/10.1111/1095-9270.12004&#xA;                  &#xA;                " href="/article/10.1007/s11457-022-09339-y#ref-CR44" id="ref-link-section-d69796620e814">2013</a>)</p>
                  
                </li>
                <li>
                  <span class="u-custom-list-number">(2)</span>
                  
                    <p>Unpublished material (Carayon <a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 2008" title="Carayon N (2008) Les Ports Pheniciens et Puniques. Geomorphologie et Infrastructure. PhD Thesis" href="/article/10.1007/s11457-022-09339-y#ref-CR23" id="ref-link-section-d69796620e828">2008</a>; Anbar <a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 2020" title="Anbar J (2020) The multi-functions harbor of Arwad Island on the Syrian coast in the Eastern Mediterranean" href="/article/10.1007/s11457-022-09339-y#ref-CR4" id="ref-link-section-d69796620e831">2020</a>)</p>
                  
                </li>
                <li>
                  <span class="u-custom-list-number">(3)</span>
                  
                    <p>Geological/geoarchaeological reports (Sanlaville et al. <a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 1997" title="Sanlaville P, Dalongeville R, Berniert P, Evin J (1997) The Syrian coast : a model of holocene coastal evolution. J Coastal Res 13:385–396" href="/article/10.1007/s11457-022-09339-y#ref-CR62" id="ref-link-section-d69796620e845">1997</a>; Marriner et al. <a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 2012" title="Marriner N, Goiran J-P, Geyer B et al (2012) Ancient harbors and Holocene morphogenesis of the Ras Ibn Hani peninsula (Syria). Quatern Res 78:35–49. &#xA;                  https://doi.org/10.1016/j.yqres.2012.03.005&#xA;                  &#xA;                " href="/article/10.1007/s11457-022-09339-y#ref-CR52" id="ref-link-section-d69796620e848">2012</a>).</p>
                  
                </li>
                <li>
                  <span class="u-custom-list-number">(4)</span>
                  
                    <p>Recent Very High-Resolution (VHR: &lt; 1 m) satellite imagery hosted on Google Earth (2009–2020).</p>
                  
                </li>
                <li>
                  <span class="u-custom-list-number">(5)</span>
                  
                    <p>High-resolution (~ 3 m) declassified Corona spy satellite imagery (1968–70) hosted by the open access Corona Atlas (<a href="http://corona.cast.uark.edu/">http://corona.cast.uark.edu/</a>).</p>
                  
                </li>
              </ol><p>This paper considers only sites located offshore, within the Low Elevation Coastal Zone (LECZ: land less than 10 m elevation and in hydrological connection to the coast) or within 500 m of the present coastline. This definition was chosen to capture all documented archaeological material on the seabed, the low-lying coastal plain, and also on elevated land close to the sea (e.g. coastal cliffs, steep slopes). This area contains a total of 290 documented sites. It is unlikely that this represents the full distribution of Syrian maritime heritage given the limitations of satellite remote sensing for archaeological prospection coupled with the lack (or publication) of research, particularly for offshore areas. Nevertheless, it gives a sample of the range of evidence on the Syrian littoral.</p><p>Documented sites concentrate in three main clusters—Tartus-Akkar Plain, the Jableh Plain, and the Ras Shamra area (Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/article/10.1007/s11457-022-09339-y#Fig3">3</a>a). There are several reasons for this pattern. Firstly, it is the focus of previous research. For example, the Tartus-Akkar Plain contains a number of well-known Phoenician colonies and harbour cities; Arwad (Arados), Antarados (Tartus), Marathos (Amrit) and Tabbat Al-Hamman (Renan <a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 1864" title="Renan E (1864) Mission de Phénicie. Imprimerie Impériale, Paris" href="/article/10.1007/s11457-022-09339-y#ref-CR59" id="ref-link-section-d69796620e892">1864</a>; Braidwood <a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 1940" title="Braidwood RJ (1940) Report on two sondages on the coast of Syria, south of Tartous. Syria 21:183–226. &#xA;                  https://doi.org/10.3406/syria.1940.4189&#xA;                  &#xA;                " href="/article/10.1007/s11457-022-09339-y#ref-CR14" id="ref-link-section-d69796620e895">1940</a>; Frost <a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 1966" title="Frost H (1966) The Arwad plans 1964: a photogrammetric survey of marine installations. Annales Archéologiques Arabes Syriennes 16:13–28" href="/article/10.1007/s11457-022-09339-y#ref-CR31" id="ref-link-section-d69796620e898">1966</a>). The Jableh Plain was subject to a comprehensive landscape survey in 1958–63 (Riis et al. <a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 2004" title="Riis PJ, Thuesen I, Lund J, Thomas R (2004) Topographical studies in the Gabla plain (Publications of the Carlsberg Expedition to Phoenicia 13; Det Kongelige Danske Videnskabernes Selskab Historik-filosofiske Skrifter 28). The Royal Danish Academy of Sciences and Letters, Copenhagen" href="/article/10.1007/s11457-022-09339-y#ref-CR60" id="ref-link-section-d69796620e901">2004</a>), whilst Ras Shamra contains Ugarit, the capital of a Bronze Age kingdom and one of the best-studied sites on the Syrian coast (Yon <a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 2006" title="Yon M (2006) The City of Ugarit at Tell Ras Shamra. Eisenbrauns, Winona Lake, Ind" href="/article/10.1007/s11457-022-09339-y#ref-CR76" id="ref-link-section-d69796620e905">2006</a>). Secondly, topography probably also influenced past settlement. The three clusters occur where the coastal plain is extensive. In contrast, the steep slopes north of Ras Shamra and, to a lesser extent, between Tartus and the Jableh Plain were less suitable for settlement. Nevertheless, areas with few documented sites highlight potential areas for future survey, particularly when they appear otherwise amenable for settlement, such as the low-lying plain south of Latakia. With regard to chronological distribution, Classical-period sites dominate and reflect the focus of much previous research during the Roman or Hellenistic periods (Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/article/10.1007/s11457-022-09339-y#Fig3">3</a>c), and the often well-preserved remains from these times. There is also a relatively high proportion of Bronze Age and Iron Age sites, again reflective of research interests in Ugarit, Phoenicia, and a general focus of investigation on tell sites which often contain material from these periods (Riis et al. <a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 2004" title="Riis PJ, Thuesen I, Lund J, Thomas R (2004) Topographical studies in the Gabla plain (Publications of the Carlsberg Expedition to Phoenicia 13; Det Kongelige Danske Videnskabernes Selskab Historik-filosofiske Skrifter 28). The Royal Danish Academy of Sciences and Letters, Copenhagen" href="/article/10.1007/s11457-022-09339-y#ref-CR60" id="ref-link-section-d69796620e911">2004</a>; Yon <a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 2006" title="Yon M (2006) The City of Ugarit at Tell Ras Shamra. Eisenbrauns, Winona Lake, Ind" href="/article/10.1007/s11457-022-09339-y#ref-CR76" id="ref-link-section-d69796620e914">2006</a>; Bretschneider and Jans <a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 2019" title="Bretschneider J, Jans G (eds) (2019) About Tell Tweini (Syria): artefacts, ecofacts and landscape : research results of the Belgian Mission. Peeters, Leuven" href="/article/10.1007/s11457-022-09339-y#ref-CR15" id="ref-link-section-d69796620e917">2019</a>).</p><div class="c-article-section__figure js-c-reading-companion-figures-item" data-test="figure" data-container-section="figure" id="figure-3" data-title="Fig. 3"><figure><figcaption><b id="Fig3" class="c-article-section__figure-caption" data-test="figure-caption-text">Fig. 3</b></figcaption><div class="c-article-section__figure-content"><div class="c-article-section__figure-item"><a class="c-article-section__figure-link" data-test="img-link" data-track="click" data-track-label="image" data-track-action="view figure" href="/article/10.1007/s11457-022-09339-y/figures/3" rel="nofollow"><picture><source type="image/webp" srcset="//media.springernature.com/lw685/springer-static/image/art%3A10.1007%2Fs11457-022-09339-y/MediaObjects/11457_2022_9339_Fig3_HTML.png?as=webp"><img aria-describedby="Fig3" src="//media.springernature.com/lw685/springer-static/image/art%3A10.1007%2Fs11457-022-09339-y/MediaObjects/11457_2022_9339_Fig3_HTML.png" alt="figure 3" loading="lazy" width="685" height="981"></picture></a></div><div class="c-article-section__figure-description" data-test="bottom-caption" id="figure-3-desc"><p><b>a</b> Spatial distribution of documented sites. <b>b</b> Bar chart showing number of occurrences of interpreted site types using all certainty qualifiers (black) and only high (definite, high, medium) certainty qualifiers (grey). Only categories with &gt; 5 documented occurrences are shown. <b>c</b> Bar chart showing number of occurrences of cultural periods using all certainty qualifiers (black) and only high certainty (Definite, Probable) qualifiers (grey). Note that the totals for B and C are more than the number of documented sites (290) because individual sites can have multiple interpretations and cultural periods. All terms used and age ranges are as per the EAMENA database controlled vocabulary</p></div></div><div class="u-text-right u-hide-print"><a class="c-article__pill-button" data-test="article-link" data-track="click" data-track-label="button" data-track-action="view figure" href="/article/10.1007/s11457-022-09339-y/figures/3" data-track-dest="link:Figure3 Full size image" aria-label="Full size image figure 3" rel="nofollow"><span>Full size image</span><svg width="16" height="16" focusable="false" role="img" aria-hidden="true" class="u-icon"><use xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xlink:href="#icon-eds-i-chevron-right-small"></use></svg></a></div></figure></div><p>The documented record contains a wide range of archaeological site types but is dominated by a few categories (Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/article/10.1007/s11457-022-09339-y#Fig3">3</a>b), the most common being quarry sites. These generally comprise regular to rectilinear features cut into coastal and foreshore bedrock and which are identifiable on satellite imagery. They cluster near Jableh, Latakia, Arwad, and Ras Ibn Hani where low sandstone or limestone platforms are exposed. Some, but not all, of these features have been identified from ground survey (Riis et al. <a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 2004" title="Riis PJ, Thuesen I, Lund J, Thomas R (2004) Topographical studies in the Gabla plain (Publications of the Carlsberg Expedition to Phoenicia 13; Det Kongelige Danske Videnskabernes Selskab Historik-filosofiske Skrifter 28). The Royal Danish Academy of Sciences and Letters, Copenhagen" href="/article/10.1007/s11457-022-09339-y#ref-CR60" id="ref-link-section-d69796620e952">2004</a>; Yon <a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 2006" title="Yon M (2006) The City of Ugarit at Tell Ras Shamra. Eisenbrauns, Winona Lake, Ind" href="/article/10.1007/s11457-022-09339-y#ref-CR76" id="ref-link-section-d69796620e955">2006</a>), but detailed investigation seems to be rare. Their locations may have been chosen to take advantage of exposed rock platforms and also facilitate transport of excavated blocks by ship. Their foreshore locations also could either indicate that they served a dual role, first functioning as quarries and then later repurposed for salt production or fish tanks (hence the high proportion of salt processing interpretations: Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/article/10.1007/s11457-022-09339-y#Fig3">3</a>b); or were submerged by RSL rise. Either interpretation would require ground investigation to confirm.</p><p>Settlement sites and tells are also common, as would be expected if the coastline was occupied during antiquity, whilst high numbers of ports and harbours have also been identified. This includes known ancient harbours (e.g. Arwad, Minet el-Beida, Tabbat al-Hamman: Carayon (<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 2008" title="Carayon N (2008) Les Ports Pheniciens et Puniques. Geomorphologie et Infrastructure. PhD Thesis" href="/article/10.1007/s11457-022-09339-y#ref-CR23" id="ref-link-section-d69796620e965">2008</a>)), as well as sheltered bays (including now-silted up examples) which could represent potential natural harbours. However, relatively few sites have built harbour infrastructure (represented in Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/article/10.1007/s11457-022-09339-y#Fig3">3</a>b by the Pier/Jetty/Wharf/Quay category). These are mainly known from previous research (e.g. Braidwood <a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 1940" title="Braidwood RJ (1940) Report on two sondages on the coast of Syria, south of Tartous. Syria 21:183–226. &#xA;                  https://doi.org/10.3406/syria.1940.4189&#xA;                  &#xA;                " href="/article/10.1007/s11457-022-09339-y#ref-CR14" id="ref-link-section-d69796620e971">1940</a>; Carayon <a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 2008" title="Carayon N (2008) Les Ports Pheniciens et Puniques. Geomorphologie et Infrastructure. PhD Thesis" href="/article/10.1007/s11457-022-09339-y#ref-CR23" id="ref-link-section-d69796620e974">2008</a>; Marriner et al. <a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 2012" title="Marriner N, Goiran J-P, Geyer B et al (2012) Ancient harbors and Holocene morphogenesis of the Ras Ibn Hani peninsula (Syria). Quatern Res 78:35–49. &#xA;                  https://doi.org/10.1016/j.yqres.2012.03.005&#xA;                  &#xA;                " href="/article/10.1007/s11457-022-09339-y#ref-CR52" id="ref-link-section-d69796620e977">2012</a>), and the low numbers could be reflective of either the general lack of maritime archaeological research in Syria or even the ready availability of natural harbours in certain locations. The Jableh Plain is for example characterized by numerous small bays and creeks that offer sheltered anchorages and shelving beaches for landing (Riis et al. <a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 2004" title="Riis PJ, Thuesen I, Lund J, Thomas R (2004) Topographical studies in the Gabla plain (Publications of the Carlsberg Expedition to Phoenicia 13; Det Kongelige Danske Videnskabernes Selskab Historik-filosofiske Skrifter 28). The Royal Danish Academy of Sciences and Letters, Copenhagen" href="/article/10.1007/s11457-022-09339-y#ref-CR60" id="ref-link-section-d69796620e981">2004</a>), whilst the reef at Arwad creates an extensive natural anchorage (Anbar <a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 2020" title="Anbar J (2020) The multi-functions harbor of Arwad Island on the Syrian coast in the Eastern Mediterranean" href="/article/10.1007/s11457-022-09339-y#ref-CR4" id="ref-link-section-d69796620e984">2020</a>). The range of other common site types is reflective of general human activity in the coastal zone, for instance cemeteries, bridges, and fortifications, and are not necessarily maritime specific.</p><p>In total, 27 shipwrecks were identified. However, only five of these are demonstrably ancient (Classical period up to 12-13th C AD: Murai et al. <a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 1988" title="Murai S, Kakiuchi H, Hano K et al (1988) Computer generated mosaic of underwater photographs of ancient amphorae, offshore the Syria. In: Murai S (ed) XVIth ISPRS Congress Technical Commission V: Other Non-Cartographic Applications of Photogrammetry and Remote Sensing. International Society for Photogrammetry and Remote Sensing, Kyoto, pp 409–419" href="/article/10.1007/s11457-022-09339-y#ref-CR55" id="ref-link-section-d69796620e990">1988</a>; Kampbell <a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 2013" title="Kampbell SM (2013) Shipwrecks of the Syrian coast. Int J Naut Archaeol 42:419–425. &#xA;                  https://doi.org/10.1111/1095-9270.12004&#xA;                  &#xA;                " href="/article/10.1007/s11457-022-09339-y#ref-CR44" id="ref-link-section-d69796620e993">2013</a>) and at least three are represented by dispersed cargo scatters rather than wreck structures and/or amphora mounds. The majority of documented wrecks are late 19th to mid-20th century AD vessels and include several large, probably metal, shallow water, intertidal wrecks with unknown construction or loss dates and which are visible on satellite imagery (Westley et al. <a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 2018" title="Westley K, Carayon N, Breen C, Blue L (2018) Benchmarking the Maritime Cultural Heritage of Syria. Unpublished report prepared for the Honor Frost Foundation" href="/article/10.1007/s11457-022-09339-y#ref-CR72" id="ref-link-section-d69796620e996">2018</a>; Wrecksite.eu <a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 2020" title="Wrecksite.eu (2020) Wrecksite.eu. &#xA;                  https://www.wrecksite.eu/&#xA;                  &#xA;                . Accessed 8 Jan 2019" href="/article/10.1007/s11457-022-09339-y#ref-CR74" id="ref-link-section-d69796620e999">2020</a>). Again, this is evidence of a lack of active underwater archaeological investigation (Haldane <a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 1993" title="Haldane D (1993) At the crossroads of history: nautical archaeology in Syria. Inst Nautical Archaeol Quarterly 20:7–11" href="/article/10.1007/s11457-022-09339-y#ref-CR37" id="ref-link-section-d69796620e1002">1993</a>; Kampbell <a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 2013" title="Kampbell SM (2013) Shipwrecks of the Syrian coast. Int J Naut Archaeol 42:419–425. &#xA;                  https://doi.org/10.1111/1095-9270.12004&#xA;                  &#xA;                " href="/article/10.1007/s11457-022-09339-y#ref-CR44" id="ref-link-section-d69796620e1006">2013</a>). If so, given the presence of numerous ancient ports and harbours, it suggests a strong possibility that many ancient shipwrecks are still to be discovered in Syrian waters.</p></div></div></section><section data-title="Threat and Disturbance Assessment"><div class="c-article-section" id="Sec6-section"><h2 class="c-article-section__title js-section-title js-c-reading-companion-sections-item" id="Sec6">Threat and Disturbance Assessment</h2><div class="c-article-section__content" id="Sec6-content"><p>Disturbance assessments were conducted primarily by comparing satellite imagery from multiple timesteps to identify impacts which have damaged sites or their surroundings. Threat assessments were then produced by extrapolating from the disturbance patterns (Rayne et al. <a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 2017" title="Rayne L, Bradbury J, Mattingly D et al (2017) From Above and on the ground: geospatial methods for recording endangered archaeology in the Middle East and North Africa. Geosciences 7:100. &#xA;                  https://doi.org/10.3390/geosciences7040100&#xA;                  &#xA;                " href="/article/10.1007/s11457-022-09339-y#ref-CR57" id="ref-link-section-d69796620e1017">2017</a>; Andreou et al. <a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 2020" title="Andreou G, Blue L, Breen C et al (2020) Maritime endangered archaeology of the Middle East and North Africa: the MarEA project. Antiquity 94:e36. &#xA;                  https://doi.org/10.15184/aqy.2020.196&#xA;                  &#xA;                " href="/article/10.1007/s11457-022-09339-y#ref-CR5" id="ref-link-section-d69796620e1020">2020</a>). For instance, a site located on the foreshore that has previously been affected by coastal erosion would then also have coastal erosion identified as a future threat. Similarly, a site located in a field with crops would have ploughing identified as a potential threat. For both threats and disturbances, certainty qualifiers (definite, high, medium, or low for disturbances; possible or probable for threats) are used to add additional nuance to the interpretation.</p><p>A general picture of past disturbance and future threat can be obtained by using general overarching categories (Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/article/10.1007/s11457-022-09339-y#Fig4">4</a>). This clearly shows that, unlike elsewhere in Syria (Danti <a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 2015" title="Danti M (2015) Ground-Based Observations of Cultural Heritage Incidents in Syria and Iraq. Near Eastern Archaeol 78:132–141. &#xA;                  https://doi.org/10.5615/neareastarch.78.3.0132&#xA;                  &#xA;                " href="/article/10.1007/s11457-022-09339-y#ref-CR26" id="ref-link-section-d69796620e1029">2015</a>; Casana and Laugier <a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 2017" title="Casana J, Laugier EJ (2017) Satellite imagery-based monitoring of archaeological site damage in the Syrian civil war. PLoS ONE 12:e0188589. &#xA;                  https://doi.org/10.1371/journal.pone.0188589&#xA;                  &#xA;                " href="/article/10.1007/s11457-022-09339-y#ref-CR24" id="ref-link-section-d69796620e1032">2017</a>; Danti et al. <a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 2017" title="Danti M, Branting S, Penacho S (2017) The American schools of oriental research cultural heritage initiatives: monitoring cultural heritage in Syria and Northern Iraq by geospatial imagery. Geosciences 7:95. &#xA;                  https://doi.org/10.3390/geosciences7040095&#xA;                  &#xA;                " href="/article/10.1007/s11457-022-09339-y#ref-CR27" id="ref-link-section-d69796620e1035">2017</a>; Kanjou <a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 2020" title="Kanjou Y (2020) The role of Syrian archaeologists and Foreign archaeological missions in the protection of the Syrian heritage. In: Otto A, Herles M, Kanuith K (Eds.) Proceedings of the 11th International Congress on the Archaeology of the Ancient Near East Volume 1.
